<p>444 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<p>toluidine blue, crystal violet, methyl violet and thionin. But the commonly used ones are the toluidine blue, methylene blue and thionin.</p>
<p>This phenomenon depends on (1) the dye and (2) the nature of the tissue parts that will pick up the dye and show metachromasia. The original colour of the dye is as a result of the single molecular form in solution. These dyes do polymerise, and as more and more polymers (dimers and trimers) are formed, the solution changes colour. For example, toluidine blue in the simple molecular form in solution is blue. When the dimers and trimers increase in number, it turns violet and eventually the full red metachromic colour appears. This is due to the polymers of the basic dye molecule. The dyes are cationic, and it is believed that all tissue components that exhibit metachromasia are composed of large anionic molecules that contain many sulphate, phosphate or carboxylic acid radicals.</p>
<p><strong>Note:</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Use 0.1% or less concentration of the dye in 30% ethanol or as 0.1% to 1% aqueous solution.</li>
<li>Do not overstain in order not to lose the metachromatic effects, for most methods staining time is 1/2–1 minute.</li>
<li>Beware of impurities that are present in some dyes (e.g., thionin) as these may stain different colours which can be mistaken for metachromasia.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Negative staining</strong> This is the method employed to examine bacterial morphology and demonstrate any envelope or capsule around the organism. The organism and the staining agent are mixed on a slide, and examined microscopically. The unstained structures are sharply seen against a dark background.</p>
<p><strong>STAINING EQUIPMENTS AND MATERIALS</strong></p>
<p>Staining of histology slides is generally done on a specially designated bench with an appropriate sink. The sink should be shallow, about 15 cm deep; and should have an efficient overflow. A slide washing tray, usually of stainless steel, may be placed in the sink. The sink must be kept clean.</p>
<p>Within the staining area, there should be a bunsen burner to heat up stains, a thermostatically controlled hot plate for melting wax around the sections and for hardening mounting media, a 37°C incubator and a 60°C oven. There should also be a microscope for controlling the degree of staining reaction.</p>
<p>The histology slides may be stained in any of the following ways:</p>
<p>(i) using staining dishes
(ii) using staining racks
(iii) using a staining machine</p>
<p><strong>Staining dishes</strong> These dishes are available in a variety of sizes. The small grooved Coplin jars with glass lids are used to stain small number of slides, usually 5–10 slides. Large staining troughs with separate transfer baskets or racks enable up to 20 slides to be stained at the same time.</p>
<p><strong>Staining racks</strong> Staining racks are usually made of two pieces of stout glass rods, about 2–4 cm apart and fastened at the ends with rubber tubing or specially designated and adjustable clips. The racks are laid on the bench across the sink. The slides are placed across the rods and stained individually by applying stains and reagents from dropping bottles or pipettes. The racks are ideal for prolonged staining procedures.</p>
<p><strong>Staining machines</strong> Staining machines follow the same general pattern of tissue processing machine but instead of tissue containers they carry slide racks. They can be programmed for varying staining periods. These machines have become standard equipments in most histopathology and cytology laboratories.</p>
<p><strong>Staining frozen sections</strong> Frozen sections may be stained individually by being transferred from one dish of reagent to the other with a hockey-stick (a tapered glass rod bent at one end to form an angle of 60°). The sections are attached to the slide at the completion of staining. Sections can also be attached to the slide before staining.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Theory of Staining 445</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ead6b0d1">♦ METHODOLOGY OF STAINING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ead6b0d1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Paraffin sections, after they have been cut, are attached on to the slides and stained. Before staining, certain preparatory treatment of the sections is necessary and involves the following steps:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Removal of paraffin wax (dewaxing) Because paraffin wax is not permeable to stains, it is removed with xylene. Two or three minutes immersion in each of two changes of xylene is usually sufficient for sections up to ten microns thick. This step can be facilitated by first warming the slide in a 60°C oven until the wax just begins to melt.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Removal of xylene Xylene is not miscible with watery solutions and low grade alcohol. It is therefore necessary to remove it with absolute alcohol. One or two minutes in each of the two changes of absolute alcohol is adequate.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Gradual hydration with lower grade alcohol Following treatment of the sections in absolute alcohol, the sections are immersed for a minute or two each in 90% and 70% alcohols. This is to avoid the possibility of diffusion currents bringing damage to, and perhaps detachment of the sections.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hydration with water The sections are now rinsed thoroughly in distilled water or tap water. After this step, the sections are ready to be stained. All the procedures listed above are usually described as 'de-wax and hydrate' or 'section down to water'. In case of stains such as Weigert's elastic stain, it is usual to take the sections from the grade of alcohol nearest to the stain which in the case of Weigert's is absolute alcohol. These steps are common to most staining techniques involving paraffin wax sections. Deviations from the steps will be indicated in the methodologies to be followed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Removal of artifact pigment Artifact pigments may be present following fixation in formalin or mercuric chloride. They should be removed by the standard methods of saturated picric acid to remove formalin pigment or alcoholic iodine and hypo treatment to remove mercuric chloride artifact.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4a2354a7">♦ STAINING IN GENERAL<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4a2354a7" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Staining may be carried out using a single (simple or compound) staining solution or by using two or more separate stains with washing and differentiation in between. Staining time ranges from a few minutes to several hours, starting from dewaxing of the section to mounting of the stained section.</p>
<p>Haematoxylin staining is either progressive or regressive accompanied by washing in tap water or Scotts tap water substitute or lithium carbonate (blueing). If regressive method has been employed, 0.5% acid alcohol (0.5 ml HCl in 70% alcohol) is used for differentiation, and this is followed by more washing in water before subsequent staining.</p>
<p>Just as some preparatory steps are taken before performing the actual staining, there are certain steps to be taken after staining before examining the section under the microscope.</p>
<p>Dehydration After staining, paraffin wax sections are usually mounted in a medium that is miscible with xylene or toluene. Since water is not miscible with any of these clearing agents, it becomes necessary to remove all the water (dehydration) through graded alcohols (70%, 90% and two changes of absolute alcohol) before passing the sections into two changes of xylene. Any trace of water in the section when placed in xylene will render the section opaque resulting in the loss of details.</p>
<p>Certain stains are soluble in alcohol, especially the low grade alcohols and for this reason, the sections should not be left in alcohol for more time than is necessary, usually a minute or less is sufficient.</p>
<p>Clearing in xylene One minute to two in each of two changes of xylene is usually adequate to give transparency to the section and completely remove alcohol from the section. The section should be returned to absolute alcohol if it is found to be insufficiently dehydrated.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>446 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mounting-stained-sections">MOUNTING STAINED SECTIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mounting-stained-sections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Due to the great difference in the refractive index of the glass slide, the tissue components and air, an unmounted stained section will show very little details when examined under the microscope. To obtain the best result with stained sections therefore, they have to be mounted in a transparent medium that has a refractive index close to that of the glass slide. Mounting medium is also desired to protect the stained section from physical damage, and from fading of the stain due to heat or oxidation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="qualities-of-a-good-mounting-medium-are">Qualities of a good mounting medium are:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#qualities-of-a-good-mounting-medium-are" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>It must have a refractive index close to that of glass (1.518).</li>
<li>It must be freely miscible with xylene and toluene.</li>
<li>It must be non-reactive.</li>
<li>It will not change colour or pH.</li>
<li>It will set hard without granularity or cracking.</li>
<li>It will not leak out any stain.</li>
<li>It should not cause loss of staining over long periods.</li>
</ol>
<p>Most mounting media are used for permanent preparation but a few others are temporary or semi-permanent mounting media.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="permanent-mounting-media">PERMANENT MOUNTING MEDIA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#permanent-mounting-media" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are usually resins which are either natural or synthetic.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="natural-resins">Natural Resins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#natural-resins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Canada balsam</strong> The most well known natural mounting medium is the canada balsam. It is an oleoresin collected from blisters in the bark of the balsamea (<em>Abies balsamea</em>) fir tree which is native to the eastern half of Canada. It is prepared by dissolving in xylene to form a fairly thin solution (about 40–50%). It is transparent, almost colourless in thin layers, glues firmly to glass and sets to a hard consistency without granulation. Its refractive index is 1.52.</p>
<p>The medium has two disadvantages:</p>
<ol>
<li>It darkens with age and,</li>
<li>With time, it slowly becomes acidified. This is because it oxidises xylene to toluic and phthalic acids, and this acidity makes most stains to fade. Because many attempts to neutralise the acidity have failed, the medium is now being supervised by the more stable synthetic resins.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Gum damar</strong> It is a natural resin obtained from the tree <em>Sherea wiesveri</em> found in East India. It must be purified before use. It does not change colour with age.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="synthetic-resins">Synthetic Resins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#synthetic-resins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Most of the synthetic mounting media are commercially available. The actual formulation or preparation is kept as a trade secret. But generally, they are prepared by dissolving a polystyrene in an aromatic hydrocarbon solvent to which is added a plasticiser such as dibutylphthalate or tricresyl phosphate to prevent the formation of air spaces when dry.</p>
<p>The most common synthetic mountants are:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p><strong>DPX of Kirkpatrick and Lendrum</strong> This is made of 10 g ‘Distrene 80’ (lubricant free) in 35 ml of xylene and plasticised with 5 ml of dibutylphthalate. It is a clear colourless mounting medium. DPX shrinks somewhat on drying. Its refractive index is 1.52.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Clarite</strong> This is a very popular mounting medium in North America. It is used as a 60% solution in xylene and has a refractive index of 1.544. It does not shrink.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Other synthetic mounting media employed by various workers are HSR (Horleco Synthetic Resin), Clear mount and Maleriol.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique-for-mounting-sections">Technique for Mounting Sections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique-for-mounting-sections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Following clearing in xylene, clean a coverslip of the appropriate size and place it on a white blotting paper, preferably on a sheet of Whatman No. 1 filter paper.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Theory of Staining 447</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>With a blunt force, pick up the slide carrying the section from the xylene bath, clean the ends first so that the diamond inscribed number is visible and the front of the slide (side on which the section is attached) is identified. Then wipe the back of the slide with a clean, dry, and soft dust-free cloth, and wipe the front to within 2 or 3 mm from the margin of the section.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>With a small glass rod, place the necessary amount of mounting medium on the section. (The ability to determine the necessary amount of mountant is acquired through experience.)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Quickly invert the slide and lower it onto the coverslip, applying gentle pressure. As soon as the medium comes in contact with the coverslip it spreads evenly to the edge of the coverslip and covers the whole area of the section which should still be moist with xylene.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Turn the slide over and with a mounted needle, square up the coverslip. Wipe excess medium away with the finger nail covered with a fine cloth dipped in xylene.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Finally, the slide is labelled indicating the tissue type or stain used and specimen number.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Following this, the sections are ready to be examined. Care should be taken not to move the coverslip. Usually, the slides should be placed on hot plates (50°) or in the wax oven for up to 2 hours, or better still, if time permits, at 37°C overnight. This is to harden the mounting medium.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Air bubbles are a common problem in mounting sections. This may be due to insufficient xylene left on the section. Air bubbles can also form when the xylene is too much. If the air bubbles are many, it is better to remount, but if one or two, it can be removed by applying gentle pressure on the coverslip with a mounted needle.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Coverslips come in various sizes and shapes—round, square or rectangle; 22 × 22, 22 × 30, 22 × 40 and 22 × 50 mm. This variety in sizes and shapes helps to ensure that any given stained section is adequately covered. Occasionally, one may notice cloudy areas in the mounted section. These are caused by moisture. If that is the case, then section has to be re-dehydrated. The coverslip is soaked off in xylene and the slide dehydrated in two baths of absolute alcohol, passed through two baths of xylene and then remounted. The causes of such inadequate dehydration may be due to rapid changes in passage through the alcohols.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 7</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="haematoxylin-staining-solutions-and-methods">Haematoxylin Staining Solutions and Methods<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#haematoxylin-staining-solutions-and-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Haematoxylin solutions can be classified according to the type of mordant used. The following are the common haematoxylin stains based on the mordants used.</p>
<ol>
<li>Alum haematoxylin</li>
<li>Iron haematoxylin</li>
<li>Tungsten haematoxylin</li>
<li>Lead haematoxylin</li>
<li>Molybdenum haematoxylin</li>
<li>Haematoxylin without mordant</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Alum haematoxylins</strong> These are the most routinely used haematoxylins. They produce good nuclear staining. The mordant is generally aluminium in the form of ammonium alum or potash alum. The nuclei are stained red which are converted to blue black, by washing in a weak alkali solution or tap water (blueing). Scott’s tap water is frequently used for blueing. The most widely utilised alum haematoxylins are the Ehrlich’s, Mayer’s, Cole’s and Harris’s.</p>
<p><strong>Iron haematoxylins</strong> The most commonly used iron salts are the ferric chloride and ferric ammonium sulphate (iron alum). These iron salts serve as both oxidising agents and mordants. The ferric salts oxidise the haematoxylin chemically and so should not be kept for too long. Ideally, it should be prepared immediately before use.</p>
<p>Iron haematoxylins demonstrate a wider range of tissue structures than the alum haematoxylins but are more time consuming. Examples of iron haematoxylin are the Weigerts, Verhoeff, Loyer and Heidenhain haematoxylins.</p>
<p><strong>Tungsten haematoxylin</strong> The phosphotungstic acid haematoxylin method of Mallory is the only application of tungsten haematoxylin, others being variation of this method. It is employed to demonstrate many tissue structures, but it is particularly useful for fibrin, muscle striations, cilia and glial fibres.</p>
<p><strong>Lead haematoxylin</strong> Lead nitrate is the mordant for this haematoxylin. It is commonly used to demonstrate the granules of the endocrine cells of the alimentary tract. It is particularly useful in the study of localisation of gastrin secreting cells in the stomach.</p>
<p><strong>Molybdenum haematoxylin</strong> This rarely used haematoxylin uses molybdic acid as a mordant. Its limited value is in the demonstration of argentaffin cell granules which are usually shown by other methods.</p>
<p><strong>Haematoxylin without mordant</strong> This group of haematoxylins is no longer in common use. They were useful in the demonstration of various minerals such as lead, iron and copper in tissues. The method was introduced by Mallory.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Haematoxylin Staining Solutions and Methods 449</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="preparation-of-haematoxylin-stain-solutions">PREPARATION OF HAEMATOXYLIN STAIN SOLUTIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-haematoxylin-stain-solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iron-haematoxylin">Iron Haematoxylin<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iron-haematoxylin" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-weigert-s-iron-haematoxylin-this-stain-is-widely-used-in-conjunction-with-van-gieson-s-stain-for-differentiating-muscle-fibres-and-connective-tissue">(a) Weigert's iron haematoxylin This stain is widely used in conjunction with van Gieson's stain for differentiating muscle fibres and connective tissue.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-weigert-s-iron-haematoxylin-this-stain-is-widely-used-in-conjunction-with-van-gieson-s-stain-for-differentiating-muscle-fibres-and-connective-tissue" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Solution A</strong>
Haematoxylin 1 g
Ethyl alcohol, 95% 100 ml</p>
<p><strong>Solution B</strong>
Ferric chloride, 29% aqueous solution 4 ml
Hydrochloric acid 1 ml
Distilled water 95 ml</p>
<p><strong>Working solution</strong>
Solution A 1 Vol
Solution B 1 Vol</p>
<p>Mix the solutions well to give a deep purple colour and use within 48 hours.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-heidenhain-s-iron-haematoxylin-this-is-a-very-useful-stain-for-demonstrating-both-nuclear-and-cytoplasmic-inclusions-it-is-also-used-to-demonstrate-muscle-striations">(b) Heidenhain's iron haematoxylin This is a very useful stain for demonstrating both nuclear and cytoplasmic inclusions. It is also used to demonstrate muscle striations.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-heidenhain-s-iron-haematoxylin-this-is-a-very-useful-stain-for-demonstrating-both-nuclear-and-cytoplasmic-inclusions-it-is-also-used-to-demonstrate-muscle-striations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Solution A (Mordant)</strong>
Iron alum 2.5 g
Distilled water 100 ml</p>
<p><strong>Solution B</strong>
Haematoxylin 0.5 g
95% ethyl alcohol 10 ml
Distilled water 90 ml</p>
<p>The haematoxylin is dissolved in the alcohol and then added to the water. The stain is left for 4–5 weeks to ripen. The stain is stable for a long time.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="alum-haematoxylins">Alum Haematoxylins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#alum-haematoxylins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are many haematoxylin stains which use alum as mordant.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-mayer-s-acid-alum-haematoxylin-this-is-a-general-purpose-stain">(a) Mayer's acid alum haematoxylin This is a general purpose stain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-mayer-s-acid-alum-haematoxylin-this-is-a-general-purpose-stain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Ammonium alum 50 g
Chloral hydrate 50 g
Haematoxylin 1 g
Citric acid 1 g</p>
<p>Sodium iodate 0.2 g
Distilled water 1000 ml</p>
<p>With the aid of gentle heat, the haematoxylin is dissolved in water. The sodium iodate is added, followed by the alum. The citric acid and the chloral hydrate are then dissolved. The stain is ready for immediate use as no ripening is required. This stain is stable for several months.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-ehrlich-s-haematoxylin-this-is-a-very-useful-all-purpose-nuclear-stain">(b) Ehrlich's haematoxylin This is a very useful all purpose nuclear stain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-ehrlich-s-haematoxylin-this-is-a-very-useful-all-purpose-nuclear-stain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Ammonium or potassium alum 3 g
Haematoxylin 2 g
Ethyl alcohol 95% 100 ml
Glycerol 100 ml
Distilled water 100 ml
Glacial acetic acid 10 ml</p>
<p>The haematoxylin is dissolved in the alcohol before the addition of the other ingredients. The stain may be ripened naturally when allowed to stand in a large container, loosely stoppered with cotton wool, at room temperature and exposed to direct sunlight. The flask should be shaken frequently, and ripening takes a few weeks. When a test slide gives a good staining effect, the stain is bottled and filtered before use. It is also possible to ripen or oxidise the stain and use it immediately by adding 0.3 g sodium iodate to the stain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="13f549">(c) Harris haematoxylin This is a powerful selective nuclear stain. It is widely used in exfoliative cytology as a nuclear stain because it gives sharp delineation of nuclear structures. Staining time is usually 2–5 minutes.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#13f549" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Haematoxylin 1 g
Absolute alcohol 10 ml
Ammonium or potassium alum 20 g
Distilled water 200 ml
Mercuric oxide 0.5 g</p>
<p>Dissolve the alum in hot water; dissolve the haematoxylin in absolute alcohol, and add it to the alum solution. Bring quickly to a boil and add the mercuric oxide which turns the solution dark purple. Cool rapidly under the tap. The addition of about 8.0 ml glacial acetic acid to the stain is recommended to sharpen nuclear staining. This stain, which is normally used regressively, should be pre-</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>450 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<p>pared in a flask of ample size due to the frothing that occurs when the mercuric oxide is added. It should be filtered before use.</p>
<p>(d) Cole’s haematoxylin Though not very popular, Cole’s haematoxylin gives satisfactory general staining effect. Unlike Ehrlich’s haematoxylin, it is suitable for use in sequence with celestine blue.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Haematoxylin</th><th>1.5 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1% iodine in 95% alcohol</td><td>50 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Saturated aqueous ammonium alum</td><td>700 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>250 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Dissolve the haematoxylin in warm distilled water, mix with the iodine solution. Add the alum solution and bring to a boil. Cool rapidly and filter before use.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="eosins">Eosins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#eosins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Eosins are acid xanthene or phthalein dyes. Eosin Y, eosin B, phloxine and erythrosin (which unlike other eosins, is halogenated with iodine), are the common members of this group of dyes. Eosin is derived from fluorescein and is available in two main shades—yellowish or blueish. When properly used on a well fixed section it stains connective tissues and cytoplasm in varying colour intensity and shades of the primary colour. It is most commonly employed as a contrast stain because it gives a useful differential contrast to nuclear stains. With haematoxylin, eosin is the routine counterstain in histopathology.</p>
<p>Eosin Y (yellowish) is the most frequently used and is readily soluble (44% w/v in water and 2% w/v in ethanol). Stock aqueous solution is generally made up in 5% w/v concentration, using tap water or distilled water. The alkalinity of tap water is considered to give better staining effect. A crystal of thymol or 0.25 ml of formalin is added to each 100 ml of stock to prevent the growth of moulds. Moulds may also be removed by filtration and they do not affect the stain. The aqueous stain is usually used at 1% for 30 seconds to 5 minutes, depending on the type of fixative, tissue and intensity of colour desired. The exact strength of the working solution is not critical.</p>
<p>The 1% w/v alcoholic solution is prepared by dissolving 1g eosin in 20 ml distilled water and made up to 100 ml by adding 80 ml of ethanol. The working solution may be 0.25% or 0.5% diluted with 80% ethanol. Some workers claim that the addition of 0.2 ml glacial acetic acid to the aqueous solution or 0.5 ml glacial acetic acid to the alcoholic solution enhances the intensity and selectivity of eosin.</p>
<p>Eosin is the most commonly used counterstain in haematoxylin staining methods. However, many substitutes are available, e.g., phloxine, Biebrich scarlet, erythrosin and orange G. These substitutes are prepared in similar concentrations and modes to eosin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="routine-haematoxylin-and-eosin-h-e-staining-method">Routine Haematoxylin and Eosin (H&amp;E) Staining Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#routine-haematoxylin-and-eosin-h-e-staining-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The haematoxylin and eosin (H&amp;E) staining technique is performed to demonstrate the general structure of tissues. It is the recommended routine stain to be done on all tissues.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Take section down to water (Refer to Chapter 6).</li>
<li>Stain in haematoxylin solution for 5–10 minutes.</li>
<li>Rinse in water for a few seconds.</li>
<li>Differentiate in 1% acid alcohol with continuous agitation for 10–15 seconds.</li>
<li>Wash in running tap water (blueing) for 5 minutes.</li>
<li>Stain in 1% aqueous eosin solution for 5 minutes.</li>
<li>Wash in running tap water for 30 seconds.</li>
<li>Dehydrate, clear and mount.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="result">Result<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#result" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nuclei</th><th>bright blue</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Cytoplasm, collagen</td><td>pale pink</td></tr><tr><td>Muscle, keratin and colloid</td><td>bright pink</td></tr><tr><td>Erythrocytes</td><td>orange-red</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="note">Note<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#note" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>To stain many slides, arrange them in a suitable bottomless glass slide carrier, one slide in each slot.</li>
<li>Alcoholic eosin solution can also be used.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 8</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="staining-reactions-of-carbohydrates">Staining Reactions of Carbohydrates<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#staining-reactions-of-carbohydrates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The most important groups of carbohydrates in histology are the polysaccharides and the mucins. The only polysaccharides widely distributed in animal tissues are the glycogens; starch and cellulose are present in plants. Glycogens are composed of chains of glucose units; they are labile and are converted to sugars soon after tissue death due to the action of glycogenolytic enzymes. They are normally stored in the liver, heart and striated muscles. In tissues, glycogens are present in a mixture with proteins. For this reason any good protein fixative will protect the glycogen with a protein membrane and so prevent the loss of glycogen after death. Glycogens are very soluble in water and theoretically, only alcoholic fixatives are suitable for fixing them. But in practice, alcohol has its disadvantages: (i) it penetrates poorly so that only the glycogens on the surface are fixed (ii) causes polarisation of glycogen (streaming) to one corner of the cell (iii) causes glycogen to appear as large coarse granules.</p>
<p>Glycogens can be preserved in paraffin sections. The action of the glycogenolytic enzymes can be slowed by cooling. Therefore, it is essential that, irrespective of what fixative is used, thin slices (about 2 mm in thickness) of tissues should be taken as soon after death as possible and placed in the fixative immediately at 4 °C.</p>
<p>The mucins form a large group of different compounds. They are normally found as mixture of compounds. The term mucin was used to describe tissues or secretions from glands which are stainable with mucin carmine when precipitated by alcohol and acetic acid. Mucins are composed of hexosamines. It is now a common practice to include mucins within the mucoid carbohydrates:</p>
<p>(i) Mucopolysaccharide — a complex consisting of polysaccharides and proteins with the carbohydrate predominating.
(ii) Mucoprotein — a polysaccharide-protein complex with the protein predominating.
(iii) Mucolipid — a polysaccharide-fat complex</p>
<p>Histochemistry has greatly helped to properly stain and identify some of these substances so that the term ‘mucin’ is becoming obsolete.</p>
<p>It is safe to say that the histological and histochemical knowledge of these carbohydrates has contributed in no small measure in understanding tissue structures. Both the glycogens and mucins are stained by the Periodic Acid Schiff (PAS) method.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="periodic-acid-schiff-pas-reaction">PERIODIC ACID SCHIFF (PAS) REACTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#periodic-acid-schiff-pas-reaction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The most important staining method for carbohydrates is based on the periodic acid Schiff (PAS) reaction. This reaction is a true histochemical method. It has its roots in the original Feulgen re</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>452 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<p>action during which hydrolysis of carbohydrates with hydrochloric acid liberates aldehydes which restore the colour of Schiff reagent. In 1866, H. Schiff showed that aldehydes recoloured basic fuchsin that has been rendered colourless (leucofuchsin) by sulphur dioxide. In 1946, McManus applied the periodic acid <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mn>4</mn></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathrm{HIO}_4)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">HIO</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">4</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> in place of the hydrolysis of the Feulgen reaction. The periodic acid produces aldehydes by oxidation of carbohydrates to restore the colour of the Schiff reagent. It was Hotchkiss in 1948 who further developed this reaction into a histochemical method for carbohydrates.</p>
<p>Basic fuchsin is a compound comprising three dyes: rosaniline, pararosaniline and magenta II. H Schiff demonstrated that aldehydes restored the magenta colour to basic fuchsin that had been decolourised by sulphur dioxide. The decolourised fuchsin (leucofuchsin) is colourless because its chromophoric bond (i.e. its quinonoid structure) has been broken. This bond and the colour can be restored by (i) aldehydes or (ii) by slow exposure to air and light.</p>
<p>Since carbohydrates in tissues may be soluble or unstable, an accurate PAS reaction very much depends on satisfactory fixation or freshly prepared cryostat section. The PAS reaction is histochemically specific but not diagnostic since many other substances or components of tissues give positive reaction. The value of the PAS reaction as a diagnostic tool is enhanced when it is used in conjunction with enzymatic or chemical controls, blocking techniques or with additional staining methods. Some other substances that are PAS positive include cartilaginous matrix, the granules of megakaryocytes, phospholipids, and colloid of thyroid gland.</p>
<p>Periodic acid is generally used as 0.5–1.0% aqueous solution for about 4–8 minutes at room temperature. Staining beyond 10 minutes usually results in tissue acidification but may also be advantageous if glycolipids, phospholipids and unsaturated lipids are being stained. This will necessitate the treatment of a control section for about five minutes. Temperature of reaction is because below <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>25</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">25^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span> so as to avoid the oxidation of aldehydes and other groups.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="63dffc65">♦ PERIODIC ACID-SCHIFF METHOD<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#63dffc65" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="preparation-of-schiff-reagent">Preparation of Schiff Reagent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-schiff-reagent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="1-itikanen-and-ogura-method">1. Itikanen and Ogura Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-itikanen-and-ogura-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>(i) Dissolve 1g basic fuchsin in <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>200</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">200\mathrm{ml}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord">200</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ml</span></span></span></span></span></span> boiling distilled water.
(ii) Cool and filter
(iii) Bubble sulphur dioxide <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathrm{SO}_2)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">SO</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> gas slowly through the solution through a fitted glass filter with occasional shaking.
(iv) Turn off the gas when the solution becomes clear transparent red.
(v) Stopper the flask and allow to stand overnight in the dark at room temperature.
(vi) If the solution is pale straw coloured or colourless next morning, it is ready for use. If it is still faintly red in colour, decolorize it by adding 1g of activated charcoal, shake and filter. If still red, discard it.
(vii) Filter into a stock brown bottle and keep in the refrigerator.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="2-de-tomasi-coleman-method">2. De Tomasi-Coleman Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-de-tomasi-coleman-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>

(iii) To the warm filtrate, add <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>20</mn><mrow><mi class="mord">1</span><span class="mord mathrm">M</span></span></span></span></span> HCl (98.3 ml HCL, Sp.gr. 1.16), make up to <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span (v) Allow to stand in the dark for about 15 hours. The solution changes to orange or straw colour.
(vi) Add 2 g activated charcoal, shake and filter into a brown stock bottle.</p>

<p>(vii) Store in the refrigerator.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-barger-and-delamoter-method">3. Barger and Delamoter Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-barger-and-delamoter-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) Dissolve 1g basic fuschsin in 400 ml boiling distilled water.
(ii) Cool to 50°C and filter.
(iii) Add 1 ml thionyl chloride (SOCl₂) to the filtrate; stopper, shake and then let stand for 12 hours in the dark.
(iv) Add 2 g activated charcoal, shake for 1 minute and filter into a brown stock bottle.
(v) Store in the refrigerator.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Thionyl reacts with water to form sulphur dioxide</li>
</ol>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mi>S</mi><mi>O</mi><mi>C</mi><msub><mi>l</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>H</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mi>O</mi><mo>⟶</mo><mi>S</mi><msub><mi>O</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>+</mo><mn>2</mn><mi>H</mi><mi>C</mi><mi>l</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">SOCl_2 + H_2O \longrightarrow SO_2 + 2HCl</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8444em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">SOC</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.01968em;">l</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: -0.0197em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">+</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.08125em;">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: -0.0813em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">O</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">⟶</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05764em;">S</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.02778em;">O</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: -0.0278em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">+</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.08125em;">H</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.01968em;">Cl</span></span></span></span></span></div>
<ol start="2">
<li>Schiff's reagent is most active when prepared fresh, so it is best prepared once a month even though the reagent may be stable for up to 4–6 months.</li>
<li>Always run control sections in parallel to test the reactivity of the reagent.</li>
<li>Reactivity is also tested by adding a few drops of the Schiff's reagent to about 4 ml of formalin on a watchglass; a good reagent will cause a rapid development of reddish purple colour while a delayed development of bluish purple suggests that the reagent is fading.</li>
<li>A reducing sulphite (sulphurous acid) rinse may be used to remove excess leucofuchsin which may be recoloured (on oxidation) and so produce false positive staining of some structures. This happens in alcoholic PAS reaction. A 10 minute wash in running tap water is a good substitute.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sulphite-rinse">Sulphite rinse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sulphite-rinse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>10% sodium or potassium metabisulphite 9.0 ml
1% hydrochloric acid 7.5 ml
Distilled water 150.0 ml</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pas-staining-techniques">PAS staining techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pas-staining-techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="sections">Sections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Paraffin, frozen or cryostat sections.</p>
<ol>
<li>Dewax and rehydrate paraffin sections. Remove mercuric pigment if necessary.</li>
<li>Oxidase for five minutes in 1% aqueous periodic acid.</li>
<li>Wash in running water for five minutes and rinse in distilled water.</li>
<li>Place in Schiff's reagent for fifteen minutes (10 minutes for frozen section).</li>
<li>Rinse for two minutes in each of three changes of freshly prepared sulphite rinse.</li>
<li>Wash in running water for ten minutes.</li>
<li>Counterstain with Harris haematoxylin for 1–3 minutes, differentiate in 1% acid alcohol, wash in tap water and blue in running water. If searching for fungi, counterstain with 0.1% light green in 0.1% acetic acid for 10–15 seconds.</li>
<li>Dehydrate, clear and mount.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>PAS positive substances : purple red (magenta)
Nuclei : blue</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="glycogen">GLYCOGEN<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#glycogen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Glycogen is a polymer of glucose. Due to its ready solubility in water it is important to fix the tissue immediately and to use thin slices of tissue. Neutral 10% formal alcohol and 10% formal saline are the fixatives of choice. Glycogen which is bound to protein becomes insoluble and is not lost during subsequent washing or staining in aqueous solutions. Acetic acid-alcohol-formalin solution (5:85:10); Bouin’s fluid and ethanol are also employed to fix glycogen. Celloidinisation of the mounted sections is not very necessary.</p>
<p>PAS reaction in conjunction with a diastase control is used by most laboratories to demonstrate glycogen. However, due to the shortcoming of the PAS reaction, the older empirical method of Best’s carmine is still considered useful. The iodine technique for glycogen is now only of historical interest.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>454 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diastase-digestion-of-glycogen">Diastase Digestion of Glycogen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diastase-digestion-of-glycogen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Diastase which is normally present in the saliva, is used to remove glycogen from sections. Malt diastase is commercially available if many slides are to be treated. The malt diastase is reliable even though it contains many other enzymes which can remove substances other than glycogen. This fact does not adversely affect the staining requirements. From practical point of view, diastase digestion makes the PAS reaction a specific test for glycogen since the glycogen is the only important PAS positive tissue component that is removed by diastase.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pas-reaction-for-glycogen">PAS Reaction for Glycogen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pas-reaction-for-glycogen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Two sections of the material under investigation are prepared; one of them which is used as a control is treated thus:</p>
<ol>
<li>Bring down to water.</li>
<li>Treat with human saliva or with 0.1% malt diastase in distilled water, freshly prepared, for 30 minutes at 37 °C.</li>
<li>Wash in running water for five minutes. The other section is left in distilled water for similar time as in step 2. Both sections are then stained by the PAS method.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Results</strong> PAS positive material that is present in the untreated section and absent in the digested section may be assumed to be glycogen.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="best-s-carmine-method-for-glycogen">Best's Carmine Method for Glycogen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#best-s-carmine-method-for-glycogen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The exact mechanism of this staining reaction is not fully understood. There is however, an empirical affinity of alkaline carminic acid for glycogen. The method, when controlled by diastase digestion, is specific for glycogen.</p>
<p><strong>Sections</strong> Paraffin sections must be covered with a thin film of celloidin before staining. This is to prevent loss of section from the slides due to the ammonical staining solution and to preserve glycogen.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="preparation-of-best-s-carmine">Preparation of Best's carmine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-best-s-carmine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Item</th><th>Stock solution</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Carmine</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Potassium carbonate</td><td>2 g</td></tr><tr><td>Potassium chloride</td><td>1 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>5 g</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Boil for five minutes in a large flask because of the marked frothing produced. Cool and add 20 ml of concentrated ammonia. Keep in the refrigerator in a dark bottle.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="working-solution">Working solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#working-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Item</th><th>Stock solution</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Best's carmine (stock solution)</td><td>2 parts</td></tr><tr><td>Ammonia (concentrated)</td><td>2 parts</td></tr><tr><td>Absolute methyl alcohol</td><td>3 parts</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="best-s-differentiator">Best's differentiator<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#best-s-differentiator" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Item</th><th>Stock solution</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Absolute methyl alcohol</td><td>40 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Absolute ethyl alcohol</td><td>80 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>100 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="celloidin-application">Celloidin application<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#celloidin-application" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Dewax the paraffin sections.</li>
<li>Take to absolute alcohol</li>
<li>Place sections in 1% celloidin in equal parts of absolute ethyl alcohol and ether for 5 minutes.</li>
<li>Wipe the back of the slide and immediately (while still wet) transfer the slide to 80% ethyl alcohol for five minutes.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="staining-method">Staining method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#staining-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Rinse the celloidinised slide briefly in water.</li>
<li>Stain with Ehrlich’s haematoxylin for 5-10 minutes and differentiate in 1% acid alcohol for 10-15 secs.</li>
<li>Quickly rinse in water.</li>
<li>Stain in working solution of Best’s carmine in a small closed jar for 10-20 minutes.</li>
<li>Differentiate in Best’s differentiator for 1-5 minutes (usually till when stain stops diffusing from the section).</li>
<li>Transfer section to absolute alcohol. The celloidin will slowly dissolve.</li>
<li>Clear in xylene.</li>
<li>Mount in a synthetic resin medium.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Item</th><th>Description</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Glycogen</td><td>bright red granules</td></tr><tr><td>Nuclei</td><td>blue</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Staining Reactions of Carbohydrates 455</p>
<p>For specificity, the test with salivary or malt diastase control as described earlier is carried out. Remember to check the potency of the stock solution when the stain gets old. The liver of a rabbit fed on carrot for three days is a suitable control though any other known positive material can be used.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="langhan-s-method-for-glycogen">Langhan's Method for Glycogen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#langhan-s-method-for-glycogen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Iodine stains glycogen as well as other compounds, most notably amyloid. Permanent preparation is difficult to produce but the following method gives good results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="sections">Sections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Paraffin sections are suitable.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="preparation-of-lugol-s-iodine">Preparation of Lugol's Iodine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-lugol-s-iodine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Iodine</th><th>1 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Potassium iodide</td><td>2 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>100 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Dissolve the potassium iodide first in the distilled water. Then dissolve the iodine in the potassium iodide solution.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="staining-method">Staining Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#staining-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Bring section to water, but wash only briefly in water.</li>
<li>Stain sections in Lugol's iodine for ten minutes. Pour off stain and dry.</li>
<li>Dehydrate in a saturated solution of iodine in absolute alcohol. Blot dry.</li>
<li>Clear rapidly in origanum oil.</li>
<li>Mount in origanum oil and ring the cover slip with vaseline or petroleum jelly.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Glycogen</th><th>:mahogany brown</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Tissue constituents</td><td>:yellow</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>This preparation fades after some months.</li>
<li>It is possible to stain nuclei with Ehrlich's haematoxylin after Stage 1.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="general-staining-methods-for-mucins">GENERAL STAINING METHODS FOR MUCINS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#general-staining-methods-for-mucins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Mucins are mucopolysaccharides or mucoproteins with varying degrees of acidification. The common staining methods for mucins include the PAS reaction and the empirical mucicarmine technique. Both connective tissue mucins and some epithelial cell mucins are well demonstrated by techniques for acid mucopolysaccharides. However, connective tissue mucins are best demonstrated by metachromatic stains. Epithelial cell mucins are well stained by Alcian blue and the PAS reaction. These two techniques are also used to demonstrate all mucopolysaccharides and mucoproteins. It should however be borne in mind that whilst neutral mucopolysaccharides and mucoproteins are well stained by PAS reaction, acid mucopolysaccharides are usually PAS negative. The PAS reaction should also be used in parallel with other methods such as Alcian blue since it is non-specific and stains other tissue components. The classical mucicarmine stain gives good results with epithelial mucins.</p>
