<li>Reticulin fibrils and fibrin are also blackened and so should not be confused with fungi.</li>
<li>A previously stained slide can easily be restained by this method as chromic acid can remove the previous stain after hydration.</li>
<li>This method is also useful for the demonstration of Pneumocystis carinii which also turns black.</li>
<li>There are several tissue elements and inclusion bodies which may resemble fungi in other stains, e.g., Gram’s and PAS methods. Constant handling of fungal sections usually creates the necessary awareness that makes the histotechnologist to be on the look out for them.</li>
</ol>
<p>♦ METHODS FOR VIRAL INCLUSION BODIES</p>
<p>The diverse group of micro-organisms known as viruses, are too small for demonstration by conventional histological procedures. Most of them are visible with electron microscopy. By direct illumination microscopy, it is possible to detect aggregates of virus particles inside host cells, and these particles are referred to as ‘viral inclusion bodies’. These inclusion bodies can be stained by various methods. They may be found in the cytoplasm (e.g., Negri bodies of rabies, Guanieri bodies of small pox (Variola), molluscum bodies of <em>Molluscum contagiosum</em> and <em>Hepatitis B virus</em></p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>480 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<p>surface antigen (HBsAg) of hepatitis B virus; or in the nucleus, (e.g., in herpes simplex, chicken pox [Varicella], and herpes zoster). They may also occur in both nucleus and cytoplasm (e.g., in cytomegalovirus [CMV] inclusion disease, measles and yellow fever). These viral inclusions stain differently and non-specifically, and so it is not possible to accurately classify or identify them by their staining reactions. The two staining methods described below only show viral inclusion bodies non-selectively as other tissue components are also stained.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lendrum-s-phloxine-tartrazine-method-for-inclusion-bodies-1947">Lendrum's Phloxine Tartrazine Method for Inclusion Bodies (1947)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lendrum-s-phloxine-tartrazine-method-for-inclusion-bodies-1947" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This method is based on the affinity of certain structures including viral bodies for phloxine. These structures retain the red stain when treated with a yellow tartrazine solution. Calcium chloride is added to the phloxine solution to intensify the stain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solutions">Solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Mayer’s haematoxylin</li>
<li>Phloxine stain</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Phloxine</th><th>1 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>70% alcohol</td><td>200 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Calcium chloride</td><td>1 g</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol start="3">
<li>Tartrazine solution</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Tartrazine</th><th>2.5 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Cellosolve (ethylene glycol mono ethyl ether)</td><td>100 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Bring section to water. Treat with iodine and sodium thiosulphate to remove mercury deposits if necessary.</li>
<li>Stain with Mayer’s haematoxyline for 5–10 minutes.</li>
<li>Blue section in running tap water for about 10–15 minutes.</li>
<li>Stain with phloxine solution for 30 minutes.</li>
<li>Rinse briefly in distilled water and drain on filter paper.</li>
<li>Differentiate with tartrazine solution until inclusion bodies are bright red and background yellow (check microscopic daily).</li>
<li>Dehydrate, clear in xylene and mount in synthetic resin medium.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Inclusion bodies</th><th>: bright red</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Nuclei</td><td>: blue</td></tr><tr><td>Background</td><td>: yellow</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; 1. Dichromate fixatives are satisfactory, but any other one is acceptable.
&gt; 2. Other structures such as fibrin, paneth cell granules and Russel bodies in plasma cells are also stained red.
&gt; 3. Negri bodies are never brightly stained.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bba32403">Schleifstein’s Method for Negri Bodies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bba32403" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This method was designed for Negri bodies of human and canine rabies. It was then described as a rapid method whereby sections could be examined less than 10 hours after the removal of the brain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solutions">Solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Staining Solution A</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Basic fuchsin</th><th>1.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Methylene blue</td><td>1.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Glycerol</td><td>100 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Methanol</td><td>100 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>This solution keeps for years</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Staining Solution B</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Potassium hydroxide</th><th>0.01 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Tap water</td><td>4000 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol start="3">
<li>Working Solution</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Solution A</th><th>10 drops</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Solution B</td><td>20 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Mix immediately before use.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Bring section to distilled water.</li>
<li>Remove mercuric deposits with iodine and sodium thiosulphate.</li>
<li>Wash well in running water.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Staining of Microorganisms in Tissues 481</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Place slides on the staining rack.</li>
<li>Flood the slides with the working staining solution. Warm the undersurface of the slide until vapour rises and leave for five minutes.</li>
<li>Differentiate each slide separately with 90% alcohol until the section is faint violet in colour.</li>
<li>Dehydrate, clear and mount in a synthetic mounting medium.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Results</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Negri bodies</th><th>: deep magenta</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Cytoplasm</td><td>: bluish violet</td></tr><tr><td>Erythrocytes</td><td>: copper red</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>STAINING METHODS FOR PROTOZOA AND OTHER PARASITES</strong></p>
<p>Staining methods for films and smears of materials suspected to contain protozoa and other parasites are by far superior to any sectional techniques. Matter of choice therefore in the study of pathogenic protozoa is the examination of blood, films and faecal specimens, even though some of these micro-organisms are found in tissues. For example, it may be difficult to obtain diagnostic material from an amoebic abscess of the liver but it will be easier and simpler to examine faeces for amoeba. Besides fresh preparation, some smears give better morphological details than sections which usually have some artefacts due to fixation and paraffin embedding.</p>
<p><strong>Demonstration Methods</strong></p>
<p>Giemsa or Leishman staining methods are useful for demonstrating malaria parasites, <em>Trypanosoma</em>, <em>Leishmania</em>, <em>Toxoplasma</em> and <em>Pneumocystis</em>. <em>Pneumocystis carinii</em> can also be demonstrated by Grocott’s silver methanamine method.</p>
<p>Amoebae (the most important intestinal protozoa) in tissue sections may be demonstrated, due to glycogen content, with Best carmine or PAS method. Phosphotungstic acid-haematoxylin will stain nuclei, chromatoid bars, and fibrils. Three methods described below give meaningful results. Parasitic worms in tissues are usually fixed whole in formal-saline or 70% alcohol to which has been added 3% acetic acid. Kirkpatrick’s carmalum method is used to demonstrate worms and their various organs.</p>
<p><strong>Gomori Trichrome Staining Technique</strong></p>
<p>This staining method is recommended for <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em> in faeces and in tissue, that is, for smear and section. It is an uncomplicated technique which gives good results with both fresh or preserved specimens. Results are obtained in less than one hour.</p>
<p><strong>Solutions</strong></p>
<p><strong>1. Schauddin’s fixative (stock solution)</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Mercuric chloride*</th><th>35 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Ethanol 95%</td><td>250 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>500 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Add the distilled water to the mercuric chloride in a heat resistant flask.</p>
<p>Place the flask in a boiling water bath to completely dissolve the solute.</p>
<p>Allow to cool at room temperature. The excess salt will crystallise out.</p>
<p>Pour the supernatant fluid into another flask and note the volume. Mix 1 part of 95% ethanol with 2 parts of the saturated mercuric chloride solution. This reagent keeps for years.</p>
<p>*Mercuric chloride is poisonous. Handle with care.</p>
<p><strong>2. Working solution</strong></p>
<p>Add 5 ml of glacial acetic acid to 95 ml of the stock Schauddin’s fixative just before use.</p>
<p><strong>3. Ethanol-iodine solution</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Ethanol, absolute</th><th>70 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>30 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Iodine crystals as needed to give brown (strong tea) colour.</p>
<p>Store in a brown well stoppered bottle.</p>
<p><strong>4. Acidified ethanol solution</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Ethanol, absolute</th><th>90 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>10 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Acetic acid</td><td>0.45 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Mix the liquids in the same order.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>482 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-trichrome-stain-chapter-9">5. Trichrome stain (Chapter 9)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-trichrome-stain-chapter-9" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Preparation</strong> Make a thin smear on a clean slide and fix in solution 1 while still wet for five minutes. Fix smears from preserved faeces for 1/2 to 1 hour (depending on the type of preservative used). Fix tissue as soon as possible, process and cut thin sections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="method">Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Place smear in 70% alcohol (or bring section to 70% alcohol) for 2–5 minutes.</li>
<li>Treat with trichrome stain for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Rinse rapidly in acidified ethanol for 3 seconds.</li>
<li>Dehydrate in absolute alcohol, clear and mount.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Cytoplasm of amoebic trophozoites and cysts : Blue green with a purple tinge
Nuclear chromatin : Purple to red
Chromatoid bodies : Purple to red
Ingested red cells : Purple to red
Background : Pale green</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iron-haematoxylin-staining-technique-spencer-monroe-method">Iron-haematoxylin Staining Technique (Spencer-Monroe Method)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iron-haematoxylin-staining-technique-spencer-monroe-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This staining method can be used with either fresh or preserved specimens. It is a standard method for a parasitologist. Fine details of amoebae and their cysts are clearly shown.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="reagents">Reagents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Schaudinn’s fixative</li>
<li>Ethanol-lodine</li>
<li>Ethanol: 70%, 95% and absolute ethanol</li>
<li>Xylene or Toluene</li>
<li>Iron-haematoxylin</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solution-1">Solution 1<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solution-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Haematoxylin 10 g
Ethanol, absolute 1000 ml
Keep the solution in a stoppered flask and allow it to ripen in sunlight for at least seven days.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solution-2">Solution 2<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solution-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Ferrous ammonium sulphate 10 g
Ferric ammonium sulphate 10 g</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="method">Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Treat with 70% ethanol for five minutes.</li>
<li>Treat with the iodine-ethanol solution for 2–5 minutes.</li>
<li>Wash in running tap water for 10 minutes, then rinse with distilled water.</li>
<li>Stain in the working solution for 2–5 minutes.</li>
<li>Wash in running tap water to blue (about 10–15 minutes).</li>
<li>Dehydrate in ascending grades of alcohol and absolute alcohol.</li>
<li>Clear in xylene.</li>
<li>Mount in synthetic medium, using no 1 coverslip.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Nuclear structures and inclusions : black
Cytoplasm, parasites and background material : shades of grey</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examination-of-whole-worms">EXAMINATION OF WHOLE WORMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examination-of-whole-worms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The parasites are washed in physiological saline to free them from tissue and intestinal debris.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="fresh-preparations">Fresh preparations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fresh-preparations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The adult parasite may be examined between a coverslip and a slide (if small); or between two slides (if large). It can also be examined in a little saline in a petri dish with the dissecting microscope.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="fixation">Fixation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fixation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>To straighten the worm, shake vigorously in a test tube of saline. Fix in 10% formal saline or in 70% alcohol to which 3% acetic acid has been added. Fixation can also be done by placing a large worm between two slides and gentle pressure applied to flatten the parasite. Secure the</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Staining of Microorganisms in Tissues 483</p>
<p>two slides with a rubber band and then fix. For small specimens, the parasite is laid on the slide, covered with a coverslip and gentle pressure applied to flatten. The preparation can be held together with a rubber band and then fixed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="kirkpatrick-s-carmalum-staining-of-whole-worms">Kirkpatrick's Carmalum Staining of Whole Worms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#kirkpatrick-s-carmalum-staining-of-whole-worms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="staining-solution">Staining solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#staining-solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Carmine</th><th>25 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Glacial acetic acid</td><td>25 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Potassium alum</td><td>25 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1000 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol>
<li>Grind the carmine.</li>
<li>Add the acetic acid.</li>
<li>Add 100 ml distilled water.</li>
<li>Leave to soak for 20 minutes.</li>
<li>Boil gently for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Allow to cool.</li>
<li>Dissolve the alum in 500 ml distilled water.</li>
<li>Add the alum solution to the carmine solution.</li>
<li>Add the remaining water.</li>
<li>Heat for 1 hour.</li>
<li>Cool and filter.</li>
<li>Preserve with thymol crystal or 1 g salicylic acid.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="method">Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Stain the worm in the staining solution for at least 12 hours.</li>
<li>Differentiate in acid-alcohol for several hours.</li>
<li>Dehydrate in ascending grades of alcohol (50%, 70% and 90%) for 30 minutes each.</li>
<li>Finally dehydrate in absolute alcohol for 4–16 hours.</li>
<li>Clear, preferably in beechwood creosote.</li>
<li>Mount in balsam.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Worm : transparent red
Various organs : shades of red</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; Mayer’s haemalum staining technique can be applied in the same manner for parasites. In this case however, 0.5% HCl is used to differentiate and running tap water for blueing. For further reading on the demonstration of ova, cysts and parasites in tissue section, please refer to any standard textbook of Histopathology.
&gt;
&gt; The adult worms, larvae or eggs of helminths in tissues can be seen in sections stained by Haematoxylin and Eosin (H &amp; E) staining.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 12
Staining of Lipids</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="demonstration-of-lipids">DEMONSTRATION OF LIPIDS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#demonstration-of-lipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Lipid is a generic term when used to describe any fatty or fat-like substance that occurs naturally and is capable of being utilised by animals and plants. It is soluble in ether, benzene, xylene, toluene and alcohols (fat solvents), but generally insoluble in water. It is therefore not easy to demonstrate in routine paraffin sections as they are dissolved during clearing. Therefore, in routine sections, for example H &amp; E stained sections, fat appears as hollow spaces in tissues.</p>
<p>There are several classes of lipids:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="simple-lipids">Simple Lipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#simple-lipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are esters of fatty acids and alcohols, e.g.,</p>
<p>(i) Neutral fats — triple esters of fatty acids and glycerol (triglycerides).
(ii) Ester waxes — esters of fatty acids and higher alcohols.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="compound-lipids">Compound Lipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#compound-lipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These consist of fatty acids, alcohol (glycerol), in addition to nitrogenous base and other compounds, e.g.,</p>
<p>(i) Phospholipids — These have phosphoric acid radical.
(ii) Derived lipids — These are formed from simple or compound lipids by hydrolysis, e.g., Fatty acids derived from triglycerides, and cholesterol derived from ester waxes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fixation-of-lipids">Fixation of Lipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fixation-of-lipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For general preservation of lipids, 10% formal saline is adequate. The addition of 1% calcium chloride and 1% cobalt chloride or cobalt nitrate to 10% formal saline gives a better result. Further treatment of the tissue with potassium dichromate (post chromatin) or with osmium tetroxide will render the fats insoluble in, and non extractable by, fat solvents. The use of carbowax embedding technique excludes the use of fat solvents and provides an excellent means of studying fat in thin sections. However, for one reason or the other in most laboratories, fats and fat embolism are still demonstrated by means of frozen section of fixed or unfixed tissues.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fat-stains">Fat Stains<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fat-stains" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The most common stains used to demonstrate fats are oil soluble dyes. This group of dyes is more soluble in the fat than in alcohol. They are used in saturated form so that their uptake by the fats is made easy. This group of dyes includes the orange-red dye Sudan III, Sudan IV, oil red O and Sudan black B. Sudan black B is the most sensitive of them. Free floating sections are to be preferred for staining because with mounted sections, particu</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Staining of Lipids 485</p>
<p>Early cryostat sections of fresh tissue, staining of fat is not usually complete as the staining tends to be superficial.</p>
<p>General solvents for these dyes are 70% alcohol, equal parts 70% alcohol and acetone, isopropyl alcohol and propylene glycol. Ideally there should be no removal of tissue lipids with 70% alcohol and the ethanol-acetone mixture. There is minimal removal of lipids with isopropyl alcohol and no removal with propylene glycol.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="staining-methods">STAINING METHODS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#staining-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sudan-black-b-in-propylene-glycol">Sudan Black B in Propylene Glycol<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sudan-black-b-in-propylene-glycol" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solutions">Solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Sudan black B solution</li>
</ol>
<p>Dissolve 0.78 g of Sudan black B in 100 ml propylene glycol. Add small amount at a time with constant stirring. Heat to 100°C for a few minutes, stirring constantly. Filter through Whatman No. 2 filter paper. Cool to room temperature and then filter again. Store at 60°C in the oven. The stain will keep for years. If precipitate forms in Sudan black B, remove it by skimming the surface with a piece of thin paper.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Propylene glycol (85%)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nuclear fast red</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Dissolve 0.1 g of nuclear fast red in 5% aqueous solution of aluminium sulphate with the aid of heat. Cool, filter and add a grain of thymol as a preservative.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Kaiser's glycerine</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Gelatin</th><th>10 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water (warm)</td><td>52.5 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Glycerol</td><td>62.5 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Phenol</td><td>1.25 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>After mixing, place small aliquots in brown bottles, melt as needed for use.</p>
<p>It is also available commercially.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Dehydrate section in absolute propylene glycol for 10–15 minutes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Stain in Sudan black B for 10 minutes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Differentiate in 85% propylene glycol.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wash in distilled water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Counterstain in nuclear fast red for 1 minute.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wash thoroughly in several changes of distilled water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Mount in glycerol gelatin.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Fat : blue-black
Background : red</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Cut frozen section at 10 µm.</li>
<li>Any other nuclear stain such as Mayer's haemalum can be used as counterstain.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="oil-red-o-supersaturated-in-isopropanol-method-little">Oil Red O Supersaturated in Isopropanol Method (Little)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#oil-red-o-supersaturated-in-isopropanol-method-little" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This method is ideal for neutral fats.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solutions">Solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Stock solution</li>
</ol>
<p>Saturated solution of oil red O (300 mg/dl) in 99% isopropanol.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Working solution</li>
</ol>
<p>Mix six parts of stock oil red O with four parts of distilled water. Allow to stand for 10 minutes and then filter. This solution should be used within 2–4 hours. Keep in air-tight staining vessels to prevent precipitation of the dye as a result of evaporation.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Mayer's or Ehrlich's or Harris's haematoxylin.</li>
<li>0.05% lithium or sodium carbonate.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Rinse sections well in distilled water.</li>
<li>Stain in oil red O for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Rinse in tap water. On being placed in water the sections unfold nicely.</li>
<li>Counterstain in Mayer's haemalum for 2–3 minutes or Harris's haematoxylin for 1 minute.</li>
<li>Rinse in tap water.</li>
<li>Blue in 0.5% lithium carbonate.</li>
<li>Rinse well in several changes of distilled water.</li>
<li>Float section onto a clean slide.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>486 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<ol start="9">
<li>Drain the slide but do not allow to dry.</li>
<li>Mount in glycerine jelly or any good aqueous mounting medium.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Results</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Lipids (Neutral fats)</th><th>: red</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Nuclei</td><td>: blue</td></tr><tr><td>Other tissues</td><td>: pale brown</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Cut frozen section at 10–40 μm.</li>
<li>After step 3, the section may be mounted with ease and examined if diagnosis only is required, e.g., of fat-embolism.</li>
<li>Cholesterol and its esters are not stained by this dye or do so weakly.</li>
<li>Some lipofuchsins are well stained.</li>
<li>Sudan black B can be substituted for oil red O.</li>
</ol>
<hr>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="osmium-tetroxide-method-for-unsaturated-lipids">Osmium Tetroxide Method for Unsaturated Lipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osmium-tetroxide-method-for-unsaturated-lipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Osmium tetroxide (osmic acid) is one of the oldest and traditional fat stains. The mechanism is not quite fully understood. Osmium tetroxide is normally soluble in fats and forms a black reduction complex with them. This complex (ester) so formed, will, as a result of a further chemical bonding or reaction, form another complex that is no longer soluble in the usual fat solvents. But when mounted in synthetic resins, the osmium will, due to oxidation, become decolourised with time. The method does not give a permanent preparation.</p>
<p><strong>Solution</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>1% aqueous osmium tetroxide solution.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Procedure</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Place frozen section in distilled water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Transfer section to, and allow to remain in, the 1% osmium tetroxide for 24 hours.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wash in several changes of water for 12–16 hours.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dehydrate in 70% (2 changes), 90% (3 changes) and absolute (2 changes) of alcohol.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wash well in distilled water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Mount in glycerol gelatin.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dehydrate, clear and mount in a synthetic medium.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Results</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Fat</th><th>: black</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Background</td><td>: yellow to brown</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<p>Use osmium tetroxide in a fume cupboard because of its toxic vapour.</p>
<hr>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fluorescence-microscopy">Fluorescence Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fluorescence-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This special form of microscopy can be employed to demonstrate lipids. Primary fluorescence is exhibited by the oxidation products of lipids referred to as lipofuchsins. Solutions containing phosphine 3R or 3,4 benzpyrene (fluorochromes) exhibit secondary fluorescence when the fluorochromes are dissolved in the lipids. The phosphine method is more permanent though the benzpyrene method is more sensitive. Both are useful for formalin frozen sections.</p>
<p><strong>Procedure</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Wash sections in distilled water.</li>
<li>Stain in 0.1% aqueous phosphine 3R for three minutes.</li>
<li>Rinse rapidly in distilled water and mount in 90% glycerine.</li>
<li>Examine with ultraviolet blue exciter filter and a colourless barrier filter under fluorescence microscope.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Results</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Simple lipids</th><th>: silver-white fluorescence</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Cholesterol esters</td><td>: silver-white fluorescence</td></tr><tr><td>Compound lipids</td><td>: silver-white fluorescence</td></tr><tr><td>Fatty acids</td><td>: no fluorescence</td></tr><tr><td>Soaps</td><td>: no fluorescence</td></tr><tr><td>Cholesterol</td><td>: no fluorescence</td></tr></tbody></table>
<hr>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4f9ac5ed">Schiff’s Reaction for Unsaturated Lipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4f9ac5ed" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This method demonstrates glyco-lipids (cerebrosides and gangliosides) and all other unsaturated lipids that contain groups which yield aldehydes on oxidation by periodic acid. There are several methods in this group, some use performic acid while</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Staining of Lipids 487</p>
<p>others use peracetic acid or a combination of the two to oxidise the sections. Some workers subject the frozen sections to ultra-violet light for 2–4 hours before treatment with Schiff’s reagent.</p>
<p><strong>Performic acid - Schiff reaction for unsaturated lipids</strong> This method can be used for paraffin or frozen sections of formal-saline fixed tissue.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="solutions">Solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li><strong>Performic acid</strong></li>
</ol>
<p>Add 31 ml of 30 % hydrogen peroxide and 0.22 ml of concentrated sulphuric acid to 8 ml of 90% formic acid. This gives a 4.7% concentration of performic acid in 2 hours. This acid is unstable.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li><strong>Schiff’s Reagent (Chapter 8)</strong></li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Bring section to water.</li>
<li>Oxidise with performic acid for 2–5 minutes.</li>
<li>Treat with Schiff’s reagent for 30 minutes.</li>
<li>Wash in warm running water for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Mount in aqueous mounting medium such as glycerol gelatin.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Unsaturated lipids : magenta-red</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="note">Note<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#note" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Peracetic acid (available commercially) is equally good for oxidation.</li>
<li>A control slide should be stained along with the test slide. The control slides are prepared with the aid of extraction procedures. Glycolipids are resistant to cold acetone treatment and so can be extracted with warm acetone.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nile-blue-method-for-neutral-and-acidic-lipids">Nile Blue Method for Neutral and Acidic Lipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nile-blue-method-for-neutral-and-acidic-lipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This method demonstrates neutral and acidic lipids. It involves the staining of formal-calcium fixed tissue with a Nile blue sulphate solution. The method involves both chemical and physical mechanisms since some components of the stain colour the lipids in the form of adsorption and others react chemically with lipid derivatives contained in the tissue. The staining reaction is as follows: Neutral lipids stain red with Nile red solution component (of the Nile blue). Acidic lipids also have a tendency to stain red. However, these acidic lipids are capable of forming salts with the free base present in the staining solution. This salt so formed is lipid-soluble and dark blue in colour. This dark blue colour masks the red colour with which the acidic lipids stain. Many fatty acids are stained blue with Nile blue sulphate.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solutions">Solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>1% acetic acid.</li>
<li>1% aqueous Nile blue sulphate solution</li>
</ol>
<p>Add some xylene to a small volume of 1% Nile blue sulphate solution to test for the presence of the Nile red oxazone component. If the mixture turns red within 20–30 seconds, then the solution is good, but if not, boil the 1% Nile blue sulphate in 5% sulphuric acid for 1–2 hours.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Rinse frozen section in distilled water.</li>
<li>Stain with 1% aqueous Nile blue sulphate solution.</li>
<li>Rinse quickly in distilled water.</li>
<li>Differentiate in 1% acetic acid for 30 seconds or until of a uniform colour. This step helps to remove dye bound to monolipid structures.</li>
<li>Rinse in distilled water and mount in glycerol gelatin.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Neutral lipids (triglycerides, cholesterol esters, steroids) : red to pink
Acidic lipids (fatty acids, phospholipids) : blue
Nuclei : blue
Cytoplasm : pale blue</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5dcc6144">Baker’s Acid Haematein Method for Phospholipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5dcc6144" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In order to prevent the dissolution of phospholipids, tissues are fixed in formol-calcium. The tissues are then chromated, and the phospholipids and certain other non-lipid acidic substances form chelate complexes. The explanation of the mecha</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>488 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<p>nism of how the chromate ion is absorbed by the tissue is open to debate. The most acceptable theory is that the dichromate renders the phospholipids insoluble through a polymerisation process with the final product being a complex lipid polymer containing chelated chromium. After the dichromate treatment, sections are stained with acid haematein to form dye lakes of bluish brown colour with the bound chromium. Differentiation with borax ferricyanide solution removes most of the dye attached to the non lipid substances, though certain components which stain very dark blue are not affected by the differentiation.</p>
<p>Prior to staining of phospholipids by the Baker’s acid haematein method, an extraction of lipid has to be carried out. The extracted tissue serves as a negative control during the staining procedure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5eb29f8e">Baker’s Pyridine Extraction Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5eb29f8e" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="fixation">Fixation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fixation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="bb690557">Dilute Bouin’s Fluid<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bb690557" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Saturated aqueous picric acid</th><th>50 ml</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>40% formalin</td><td>10 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Glacial acetic acid</td><td>5 ml</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>35 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ol>
<li>Fix small pieces of tissue in Bouin’s solution for 18–20 hours.</li>
<li>Wash in 70% ethanol to remove picric acid for 1 hour.</li>
<li>Wash in 50% alcohol for 30 minutes.</li>
<li>Extract with pyridine at room temperature for 1 hour.</li>
<li>Extract with pyridine at 60 °C for 24 hours.</li>
<li>Wash in running tap water for 2 hours.</li>
<li>Place in dichromate-calcium chloride solution and proceed as in step 2 of Baker’s staining method below.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="staining-method">Staining method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#staining-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="solutions">Solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ol>
<li>Fixative solution: Formol-calcium
40% formalin
10 ml</li>
</ol>
<p>10% annhydrous calcium chloride
Distilled water
10 ml</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>5% potassium dichromate containing calcium chloride
80 ml</li>
</ol>
<p>Potassium dichromate
5 g</p>
<p>Calcium chloride
1 g</p>
<p>Distilled water
100 ml</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Acid Haematein
Haematoxylin
0.05 g</li>
</ol>
<p>1% sodium iodate
1 ml</p>
<p>Glacial acetic acid
1 ml</p>
<p>Distilled water
48 ml</p>
<p>Dissolve the haematoxylin and sodium iodate in the distilled water with the aid of heat, cool, and then add the acetic acid. Prepare fresh and use same day as the solution is not stable.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Borax ferricyanide
Potassium ferricyanide
0.25 g</li>
</ol>
<p>Sodium tetraborate (Borax)
0.25 g</p>
<p>Distilled water
100 ml</p>
<p>Store in the dark, the solution is stable.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ol>
<li>Fix small pieces of tissue in the formol calcium solution for between 6 and 18 hours.</li>
<li>Transfer into dichromate-calcium solution at room temperature for 18 hours.</li>
<li>Transfer into fresh dichromate-calcium solution at 60°C for 24 hours.</li>
<li>Wash in tap water for six hours, rinse in distilled water.</li>
<li>Cut frozen sections at 10 μm.</li>
<li>Treat with dichromate-calcium solution at 60 °C for 1 hour.</li>
<li>Wash in running water for three minutes and then rinse in distilled water.</li>
<li>Stain in acid haematein at 37 °C for five hours.</li>
<li>Rinse in distilled water.</li>
<li>Differentiate in borax-ferricyanide solution at 37 °C for 18 hours.</li>
<li>Wash in tap water for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Rinse in distilled water.</li>
<li>Mount in aqueous medium.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Staining of Lipids 489</p>
<p>Result
Phospholipids : dark blue
Nucleoproteins : dark blue
Spilengolipids : dark blue
Cytoplasm : pale yellow</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>This method is neither sensitive nor specific for phospholipids, hence the use of controls.</li>
<li>The pyridine extraction method though originally designed for Baker's acid haematein method, can be followed by Sudan black B or other stains.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="methods-for-demonstrating-cholesterol">METHODS FOR DEMONSTRATING CHOLESTEROL<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#methods-for-demonstrating-cholesterol" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="polarised-light-method">Polarised Light Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#polarised-light-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Cholesterol usually exhibits birefringence when viewed with polarised light. While the birefringence seen in discrete chronic lesion may be attributed to the presence of cholesterol, other lipids, certain proteins, amyloid or even formalin artefacts may also show birefringence. The use of polarised light therefore may only indicate the presence or absence of cholesterol. It may however, be useful when performed in conjunction with extraction procedures.</p>
<p><strong>Method</strong> Unstained, formol-calcium fixed frozen section is mounted in an aqueous mounting medium and examined with polarised microscope.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="79b135f9">Schultz’s Method for Cholesterol and Its Esters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#79b135f9" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This method is based on the oxidation of cholesterol to oxycholesterol. This oxidation may be accomplished with ferric ammonium salt. A colour reaction develops when acid mixture is added to the section. This method is not totally specific for cholesterol or its esters since carotene gives a similar colour reaction if present.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="solution">Solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Iron alum solution
Iron alum 2.5 g
Distilled water 100 ml</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Acid mixture
Add concentrated sulphuric acid slowly to an equal volume of glacial acetic acid, stirring and cooling all the while.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Rinse the formalin or formol-calcium fixed frozen section in distilled water.</li>
<li>Place the section in the iron alum at 37 °C for 3 days.</li>
<li>Rinse in water, mount on a clean slide and blot gently with fine filter paper, or drain off excess fluid.</li>
<li>Add some drops of the acid mixture, carefully apply a coverslip.</li>
<li>Examine.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Results</strong> A positive test for cholesterol or its esters is indicated by the rapid appearance of blue-green colour that reaches its maximum intensity within a few minutes. Within 30 minutes, the colour changes to greenish-brown.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>It is important to use high grade acids. The use of impure acids will result in the formation of large number of bubbles which will render microscopic examination difficult.</li>
<li>Treatment of the section with 20% ferric chloride will give a more rapid oxidation.</li>
<li>The acid mixture is hygroscopic and so must be protected from atmospheric moisture.</li>
<li>A control section of adrenal cortex can be used.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="digitonin-reaction-for-non-esterified-cholesterol">Digitonin Reaction for Non-esterified Cholesterol<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#digitonin-reaction-for-non-esterified-cholesterol" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Digitonin, a naturally occurring glucoside and a precipitating agent will form crystalline complexes with non-esterified cholesterol.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>490 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="solution">Solution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>0.5% solution of digitonin in 50% alcohol.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Wash formal-saline fixed frozen sections in distilled water.</li>
<li>Immerse section in the 0.5% digitonin for several hours.</li>
<li>Rinse in 50% alcohol.</li>
<li>Counterstain one of the sections with Sudan IV or oil red O.</li>
<li>Mount all sections in aqueous mountant.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="results">Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Examination of the uncounterstained section with polarised microscope shows rosettes of digitonin-cholesterol complexes which are birefringent. In the counterstained slide, the cholesterol compound remains doubly refractile and does not stain, the cholesterol esters are stained and lose their birefringence.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; The cholesterol-digitonin crystals are insoluble in cold 95% acetone, ether or water; slightly soluble in hot ethanol and in methanol; readily soluble in glacial acetic acid, pyridine and chloral hydrate.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 13</p>
<p>The Central Nervous System (CNS)</p>
<p>The anatomy of the central nervous system (CNS) is such that structurally, the whole system is divided into nerve cells (neurones) and their processes on one hand and the neuroglia (supporting cells) on the other. Extracellular components are present in the peripheral nerves and the meninges (protective layer of the brain and the spinal cord). There are millions of nerve cells which are interconnected in a most complex but orderly manner.</p>
<p>The structures of CNS that are usually demonstrated are listed in Table 13.1.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251218-2598def2.jpeg" alt="img-38.jpeg">
Table 13.1 Structures of CNS commonly demonstrated</p>
<p>There is no one stain or combination of stains, metallic impregnation or histochemical method</p>
<p>which can demonstrate all the components of the CNS in a single preparation. For this reason, there are several and various methods for the demonstration of CNS components. A generated message is sent out by the nerve cell along its efferent fibre, the axon. The same axon receives the messages from other cells by means of short branched processes known as dendrites. The conducting fibres (axons) are 'insulated' or protected from each other. The 'insulation' is brought about by the action of a white fatty substance called the myelin, which surrounds each of the fibres. Parts of the brain and most peripheral nerves are myelinated. The abundance of myelin gives the surrounding areas and structures a whitish appearance, hence the name 'white matter'. Parts of the brain where the nerve cells are bound together have a grey colour and so they are called grey matter.</p>
<p>The nerve cells are supported by the neuroglia which form the specialised connective tissue of the CNS. Neuroglia, structurally consists of four types of cells—astrocytes, oligodendroglia, microglia and ependymal cells. A neurone is a complete cell consisting of several cytoplasmic structures including Golgi apparatus, mitochondria, inclusion bodies and Nissl substance (Nissl bodies, chromophil substances or trigoid substances), and the nucleus.</p>
<p>The responses of CNS to diseases or injury take place in the neurones and in the axons. The Nissl</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>492 Histopathological Techniques and Cytology</p>
<p>granules are lost and the nucleus moves from its central location to the periphery of the cytoplasm which begins to shrink. A myelinated fibre begins to degenerate and eventually is lost.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="f5ec3f74">♦ PREPARATION OF SECTIONS OF CNS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#f5ec3f74" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The CNS is greatly prone to autolytic changes and so prompt fixation is essential. The fixation of CNS tissues has already been described under fixations, but it is worth remembering that 10% buffered formal saline is the best general purpose fixative for the CNS.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="paraffin-sections">Paraffin Sections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paraffin-sections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The CNS tissues are dense and quite rich in lipids. They are therefore not easily penetrated by paraffin wax. It is recommended to leave large blocks in the paraffin wax bath for 24 hours. Distortion of the tissue can be reduced by using the celloidin-paraffin medium. Frozen sections of the CNS tissues are necessary in order to effect proper study of certain parts of the CNS.</p>
